### [1] [Agentic Analysis for Agentic Infrastructure: An LLM-Powered Pipeline for Comparative Governance of DAO and Corporate AI Protocols](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26203)
*Yutian Wang,Luyao Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper introduces an LLM-powered pipeline for analyzing governance structures of AI agent protocols, applying it to compare ERC-8004 (permissionless) and Google A2A (corporate-led) standards, finding differences in discourse alignment but similar participation inequality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI agent protocols proliferate, there's a lack of empirical examination of the governance structures that shape their interoperability standards. The paper aims to address this gap by studying how institutional design influences governance discourse and community structure.

Method: The authors developed an LLM-powered comparative pipeline integrating automated annotation, neural topic modeling, and multi-layer network analysis. They analyzed 4,323 governance participation records from two contrasting standards: ERC-8004 (permissionless, on-chain) and Google A2A (corporate-led). The method combines LLM-assisted coding, topic modeling, and multi-layer network analysis.

Result: The study found that while governance form influences substantive focus, both regimes exhibit comparable levels of participation inequality and community fragmentation. Discourse alignment is denser in the permissionless setting (ERC-8004), suggesting that open governance may foster greater thematic convergence despite decentralized participation.

Conclusion: LLM-assisted methods can advance the empirical study of technology governance, with implications for designing more equitable agentic AI standards. The findings demonstrate the value of computational approaches for understanding socio-technical power structures at scale.

Abstract: As AI agent protocols proliferate, the governance structures shaping their interoperability standards remain empirically underexamined. We introduce an LLM-powered comparative pipeline for large-scale governance discourse analysis, integrating automated annotation, neural topic modeling, and multi-layer network analysis to study socio-technical power structures at scale. We validate it on two contrasting standards for agent interoperability: ERC-8004 (permissionless, on-chain) and Google A2A (corporate-led). Analyzing 4,323 governance participation records, we combine LLM-assisted coding, topic modeling, and multi-layer network analysis to examine how institutional design shapes thematic priorities and community structure. We find that while governance form influences substantive focus, both regimes exhibit comparable levels of participation inequality and community fragmentation. Discourse alignment is denser in the permissionless setting, suggesting that open governance may foster greater thematic convergence despite decentralized participation. These findings illustrate how LLM-assisted methods can advance the empirical study of technology governance, with implications for designing more equitable agentic AI standards. All data and code are openly available.

</details>


### [2] [Instruction Bleed: Cross-Module Interference in Prompt-Composed Agentic Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26356)
*Ching-Yu Lin,Yifan Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Researchers identify "compositional behavioral leakage" (CBL) where editing one prompt module silently affects others in agentic systems, despite no explicit dependencies, due to transformer architecture limitations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Practitioners observe recurring failure modes in prompt-composed agentic systems where editing one prompt module unexpectedly changes behavior of other modules, even without shared variables or explicit dependencies. This interference problem lacks formal understanding and measurement tools.

Method: The study formalizes CBL as interference between modules sharing a context window, enabled by transformer self-attention's lack of formal boundaries. Researchers test CBL on a deployed job-evaluation agent (Claude Sonnet 4.6) using a three-channel protocol (volume, content, form) with 144 trials to perturb non-focal modules and measure effects.

Result: Only the content channel produced detectable paired effects (Cohen's d = 0.63, bootstrap 95% CI excluding zero), but no recommendations actually flipped - indicating sub-threshold interference that accumulates across thousands of decisions. CBL is distinct from known agent failure modes like adversarial injection or privacy leakage.

Conclusion: The paper establishes cross-module interference measurement as essential for prompt-composed agent evaluation, contributes an operational definition of CBL, a reusable testing protocol, falsifiable predictions, and system-class characterization that addresses a previously unmeasured failure axis.

Abstract: Practitioners of prompt-composed agentic systems report a recurring failure mode: editing one prompt module silently shifts the behavior of others despite no shared variable or executable dependency. We formalize this as compositional behavioral leakage (CBL): interference between modules sharing a context window. CBL is enabled by architectural non-isolation: transformer self-attention provides no formal boundary between concatenated modules. We probe CBL on a deployed job-evaluation agent (Claude Sonnet 4.6, 144 trials) through a reusable three-channel protocol that perturbs non-focal modules along volume, content, and form. Only the content channel produces a detectable paired effect (Cohen's d = 0.63, bootstrap 95% CI excluding zero); no recommendation flipped -- a sub-threshold regime invisible to standard QA but compounding across the thousands of decisions a deployed agent makes. CBL is orthogonal to known agent-failure axes (adversarial injection, cognitive degradation, multi-agent fault propagation, privacy leakage). We contribute an operational definition, a reusable protocol, a falsifiable prediction set, and a system-class characterization, establishing cross-module interference measurement as a requirement for prompt-composed agent evaluation.

</details>


### [3] [Detecting and Controlling Sycophancy with Cascading Linear Features](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26155)
*Maty Bohacek,Rishub Jain,Nicholas Dufour,Thomas Leung,Chris Bregler,Roma Patel*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: An iterative data generation method that isolates cascading linear features for better model behavior interpretation and steering, specifically applied to reduce sycophancy in language models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current activation steering methods require many contrastive sample pairs for reliable feature detection and behavior steering. Simple binary pairs limit the ability to disentangle features responsible for model behaviors.

Method: Proposes an iterative data generation pipeline that goes beyond binary contrastive samples to isolate cascading linear features that scale linearly with behavior. Specifically creates samples showing degrees of features to better disentangle them. Applied to detect and steer away from sycophancy in language models.

Result: Sycophancy features discovered through cascading samples form linearly separable subspaces, enabling clearer activation selection corresponding to desired behavior. The approach matches or outperforms LLM-as-a-judge and system prompting baselines for detection, deterministic scoring, and robust steering, while offering lower computational demand and better interpretability guarantees.

Conclusion: The cascading linear feature isolation method provides a more effective approach for interpreting and controlling model behaviors, particularly for mitigating undesirable traits like sycophancy in language models, with improved interpretability and computational efficiency compared to existing methods.

Abstract: Interpreting and controlling model behaviors through activation steering methods requires many pairs of contrastive samples that clearly exhibit desired or undesired behavior. These data pairs determine the degree to which interpretability frameworks can reliably detect model features responsible for a behavior, and therefore the ability to steer models toward or away from such behavior. In this work, we present an iterative data generation pipeline that isolates cascading linear features responsible for a behavior. Specifically, we show how moving beyond simple binary pairs of samples, and instead isolating samples that show degrees of features that scale linearly with behavior, allows for better disentanglement of features. We focus on detecting and steering away from sycophancy -- the tendency of language models to prioritize user validation. We demonstrate that sycophancy features discovered through cascading samples form linearly separable subspaces, and allow for selection of model activations that more clearly correspond to the desired behavior than baseline approaches. We also evaluate their ability to enable detection, deterministic scoring, and robust steering, and see that they either match or outperform LLM-as-a-judge and system prompting baselines while providing lower computational demand and more interpretability guarantees. Code & Data: https://cascading-feats.github.io/

</details>


### [4] [Life After Benchmark Saturation: A Case Study of CORE-Bench](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26158)
*Nitya Nadgir,Sayash Kapoor,Kangheng Liu,Peter Kirgis,Matilda Orona,Stephan Rabanser,Tilman Bayer,Abhishek Shetty,Yue Ling,Derrick Chan-Sew,Rumi Nakagawa,Saiteja Utpala,Zachary S. Siegel,Arvind Narayanan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: When benchmark accuracy saturates, instead of retiring it, we should measure six other dimensions of agent performance beyond accuracy: construct validity, out-of-distribution generalizability, efficiency, reliability, model vs scaffold importance, and human-agent collaboration uplift.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current benchmark evaluation practices privilege accuracy and retire benchmarks when accuracy saturates, missing opportunities to study other important dimensions of agent performance that remain meaningful even after accuracy saturation.

Method: Using CORE-Bench Hard as a case study, the authors: 1) Surface construct validity threats using more capable agents, 2) Create improved benchmark CORE-Bench v1.1 and OOD task suite, 3) Measure efficiency, reliability, model/scaffold performance even after accuracy saturation, 4) Conduct randomized experiment to measure human-agent collaboration uplift on real-world tasks.

Result: 1) Identified construct validity threats in CORE-Bench Hard, 2) Despite accuracy saturation, CORE-Bench v1.1 remained useful for measuring other dimensions, 3) Human-agent collaboration showed statistically significant 2x speedup (likely underestimated), 4) Presented more comprehensive evaluation paradigm beyond accuracy-centric approaches.

Conclusion: The paper presents a more rigorous alternative to accuracy-centric evaluation by proposing measurement of six additional dimensions of agent performance, demonstrating that benchmarks remain valuable even after accuracy saturates for studying important performance characteristics like reliability, efficiency, and human collaboration benefits.

Abstract: When a benchmark's accuracy saturates, it is often retired and replaced with a more challenging version. We show that this approach privileges accuracy and misses the opportunity to study six other key dimensions of agent performance: construct validity issues such as shortcuts, out-of-distribution generalizability, efficiency, reliability, the relative importance of the model versus the scaffold, and uplift from human-agent collaboration. We use CORE-Bench Hard, a benchmark for computational reproducibility of scientific code, as a case study to demonstrate that measuring agents along these dimensions yields meaningful insights into agent performance even after accuracy saturates. First, we surface threats to construct validity in CORE-Bench Hard that are difficult to anticipate with less capable agents. We introduce an improved benchmark, CORE-Bench v1.1, and an out-of-distribution task suite, CORE-Bench OOD. Second, we find that despite accuracy saturation, CORE-Bench v1.1 remains useful for measuring efficiency, reliability, model performance, and scaffold performance. Finally, we conduct a small-scale randomized experiment to measure uplift from human-agent collaboration on real-world computational reproducibility tasks. We find a statistically significant speedup by about a factor of two -- likely underestimated due to one-fifth of human-only reproductions reaching the time limit before completing -- and describe various other findings. Together, our contributions present a more rigorous alternative to the dominant accuracy-centric evaluation paradigm.

</details>


### [5] [Scientific discovery as meta-optimization: a combinatorial optimization case study](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26728)
*Yuan-Hang Zhang,Chesson Sipling,Massimiliano Di Ventra*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Researchers propose meta-optimization for scientific discovery, where both theories/experiments AND evaluation criteria are optimized simultaneously through consensus objective aggregation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scientific discovery is fundamentally an optimization problem with a vast state space of theories and experiments. While LLMs enable automated exploration of this space, current approaches fail to simultaneously optimize the evaluation criteria themselves.

Method: Formalize research as meta-optimization where the optimization objective itself is being optimized. Introduce "consensus objective aggregation" - LLM-generated objective functions are combined via correlation-weighted voting to create a stable, self-correcting evaluation criterion that evolves with understanding.

Result: Applied framework to algorithm discovery for 3-SAT problems using digital MemComputing machines. Reduced baseline scaling from ~N².⁵¹ to ~N¹.³³, achieving ~67× speedup on largest instances tested.

Conclusion: The meta-optimization framework with consensus objective aggregation represents a problem-agnostic approach that can considerably aid scientific discovery by evolving both solutions and evaluation criteria simultaneously.

Abstract: Scientific discovery is fundamentally an optimization problem, defined by a vast "state space" of theories and experiments, and an evaluation criterion based on quality, novelty, and validity. Large language models (LLMs) have enabled automated exploration of this space, but we argue that simultaneous modification of the evaluation criteria is equally important. Here, we propose formalizing research as meta-optimization, where the optimization objective itself is also being optimized. Our key contribution is "consensus objective aggregation," where LLM-generated objective functions are combined via correlation-weighted voting, yielding a stable, self-correcting evaluation criterion that evolves as understanding deepens. We apply this framework to algorithm discovery for 3-SAT problems based on digital MemComputing machines, reducing the baseline scaling with problem size $N$ from $\sim N^{2.51}$ to $\sim N^{1.33}$ and delivering a $\sim 67\times$ speedup on the largest instances tested. As a problem-agnostic framework, we hope this approach will considerably aid scientific discovery.

</details>


### [6] [Refusal Lives Downstream of Persona in Chat Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26161)
*Viola Zhong,Qirui Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Refusal behaviors in instruction-tuned chat models are gated by compliant persona directions at late layers, not isolated mechanisms.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Previous research has studied refusal and persona traits in LLMs as separate mechanisms, but their interaction remains unexplored. The authors aim to investigate whether compliant personas gate refusal behaviors in instruction-tuned models.

Method: Extracted linear directions for compliant model-persona and refusal behaviors in Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct and Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct. Performed intervention experiments by steering both directions simultaneously and observing refusal rates. Used projection techniques to analyze layer-specific effects, particularly comparing early vs late layers.

Result: Compliant persona steering strongly suppresses refusal behaviors (Llama refusal rate drops from 97% to 2%). Refusal direction restoration works at late layers but not early ones. Projecting out the persona direction in late layers restores refusal to baseline, while projecting random directions doesn't. This shows refusal is gated at late-layer expression stage.

Conclusion: Refusal behaviors in instruction-tuned LLMs are not isolated mechanisms but are gated by persona directions at the late-layer expression stage, downstream of where refusal is computed. Treating refusal as a single direction misses this critical dependency on persona traits.

Abstract: Linear directions in activation space have been identified for both refusal and persona traits in instruction-tuned chat models, but the two have been studied as separate mechanisms. We show they interact: a compliant persona gates refusal. In Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct and Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct, we extract a compliant model-persona direction and a refusal direction and intervene on both. Compliant persona steering suppresses refusal -- in Llama, the refusal rate falls from 97% to 2%. Reintroducing the refusal direction partially restores refusal at late layers but not at early ones. Projecting out the persona direction in a late-layer window restores it to baseline; projecting out a random direction does not. Refusal is therefore gated at the late-layer expression stage, downstream of where it is computed. Treating refusal as a single isolated direction misses its dependence on persona.

</details>


### [7] [Semantic Early-Stopping for Iterative LLM Agent Loops](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.27009)
*Sahil Shrivastava*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes semantic early-stopping for multi-agent LLM loops, replacing fixed iteration caps with stopping based on semantic convergence and quality improvement, reducing token usage while maintaining quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fixed iteration caps in multi-agent LLM loops are inefficient - they overspend tokens on easy inputs and truncate hard ones. A semantic early-stopping mechanism is needed to terminate loops based on actual convergence rather than arbitrary iteration limits.

Method: Proposes semantic early-stopping that halts when consecutive draft embeddings stop changing meaning (cosine distance with patience window) and answer quality stops improving. Also introduces judge-efficient evaluation protocol with trajectory replay and cached LLM-judge calls.

Result: On HotpotQA test split, judge-free semantic stopper reduces operational tokens by 38% relative to max_iterations at parity quality. Full quality-gated variant is counter-productive due to per-round judging cost. Oracle selecting best round outperforms all practical policies.

Conclusion: Semantic early-stopping effectively reduces token usage while maintaining quality, but the core problem shifts from 'when to stop' (solved) to 'which round is best' (open), as oracle performance shows significant room for improvement.

Abstract: Multi-agent large language model (LLM) loops, for example a Writer that drafts and a Critic that revises, are almost always terminated by a fixed iteration cap (max_iterations). This is a syntactic kill-switch: it is blind to whether the answer is still improving, so it over-spends tokens on easy inputs and truncates hard ones. We study semantic early-stopping: the loop halts when consecutive draft embeddings stop changing in meaning (cosine distance with a patience window) and the answer's measured quality stops improving. Our work makes three contributions. First, an honest theoretical footing: we prove deterministic termination and well-definedness and machine-check these claims, while treating the convergence of the distance sequence as an empirically tested conjecture rather than a (previously over-claimed) Banach contraction. Second, a judge-efficient evaluation protocol: we generate each question's full trajectory once, replay every stopping policy over the identical drafts, and cache every LLM-judge call, yielding a strictly paired efficiency-versus-quality comparison at low cost; we further separate operational tokens (charged to a policy) from evaluation tokens (a measurement instrument). Third, an empirical study on multi-hop retrieval-augmented question answering (HotpotQA). On the 60-question test split, a judge-free semantic stopper reduces operational tokens by 38% relative to max_iterations at parity quality (Delta-IS = -0.004, p = 0.81), whereas the full quality-gated variant is counter-productive because its per-round judging dominates cost. An oracle that selects the best round attains +0.115 Information Score over every practical policy (p ~ 4e-11), reframing the problem from "when to stop" (easy) to "which round is best" (open).

</details>


### [8] [AlgoEvolve: LLM-driven Meta-evolution of Algorithmic Trading Programs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26173)
*Dhruv Sharma,Gautam Shroff*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: LLM-driven evolutionary framework discovers and improves algorithmic trading strategies through semantic mutation and meta-evolution of prompts, outperforming human-designed instructions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While LLMs have shown promise as semantic mutation operators for evolutionary program discovery in static coding benchmarks, algorithmic trading presents unique challenges due to its noisy, non-stationary, and discontinuous nature, requiring adaptation to real-world complex environments.

Method: AlgoEvolve uses LLMs as semantic mutation operators in an evolutionary framework that generates, evaluates, and iteratively improves executable Python trading strategies. It includes a meta-evolutionary outer loop that evolves prompts guiding program synthesis in the inner loop to discover improved search heuristics.

Result: The system demonstrates emergent regime-adaptive strategy logic with autonomous shifts in trading rules. The meta-evolutionary outer loop discovers heuristics that balance exploration-exploitation, reduce zero-trade failures, and consistently outperform initial human-designed instructions.

Conclusion: LLM-based semantic evolution provides a viable approach for continual program synthesis in complex, dynamic environments like algorithmic trading, showing promise for adaptive system development in noisy domains.

Abstract: Recent work shows that Large Language Models (LLMs) can act as semantic mutation operators for the evolutionary discovery of programs and proofs. Most current applications focus on static coding benchmarks. We extend this paradigm to algorithmic trading. This domain is uniquely challenging because it is noisy, non-stationary, and highly discontinuous. We present AlgoEvolve, an LLM-driven evolutionary framework that generates, evaluates, and iteratively improves executable trading strategies. These strategies are expressed as Python code and evaluated through a rigorous testing protocol. Across multiple experiments, the system exhibits emergent regime-adaptive strategy logic, including autonomous shifts in trading rules. We further introduce a meta-evolutionary outer loop that evolves the prompts guiding program synthesis in the inner loop. This outer loop discovers improved search heuristics. These heuristics balance exploration and exploitation while reducing zero-trade failures. They consistently outperform initial human-designed instructions. The results demonstrate that LLM-based semantic evolution provides a viable approach for continual program synthesis in complex environments.

</details>


### [9] [Knowledge-augmented Agentic AI for Mental Health Medication Information Seeking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26205)
*Huizi Yu,Jian Liu,Wenkong Wang,Lingyao Li,Jiayan Zhou,Zhaoqian Xue,Xiang Li,Xinxin Lin,Zhiying Liang,Zhuoru Wu,Siyuan Ma,Xin Ma,Lizhou Fan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper presents a provenance-aware knowledge graph framework that integrates regulatory adverse-event reports and patient narratives for psychiatric drugs to improve medication safety information while preserving source credibility.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Patients increasingly seek medication information online, but psychiatric drug safety knowledge is fragmented between authoritative but abstract regulatory records and experience-near but unvalidated patient narratives. Integrating these sources without conflating evidence and anecdote is crucial in psychiatry, where poorly contextualized information can amplify fear, nocebo responses, and non-adherence.

Method: Developed a provenance-aware, knowledge-graph-based multi-agent framework that unified 466,525 Reddit posts, 60,782 WebMD reviews, and twenty years of U.S. FDA Adverse Event Reporting System records for nine antidepressants. Used a large-language-model entity-recognition pipeline benchmarked against physician annotations. Constructed a Neo4j knowledge graph grounded in ATC-N, ICD-10, and MedDRA vocabularies to preserve provenance and traceability.

Result: Entity recognition achieved F1 scores of 0.969 for medications and 0.973 for conditions. Community platforms (Reddit and WebMD) showed high concordance with each other (Jaccard similarity up to 0.905) but low concordance with regulatory reports, indicating patient-generated data form an independent safety signal. For sertraline, many adverse events appeared in community sources hundreds of days before corresponding FDA dates.

Conclusion: The provenance-aware integration establishes a route to more auditable psychiatric medication information, with source-aware integration allowing claims to be traceable and regulatory facts distinct from patient experience. The approach shows potential usefulness and patient benefit, though prospective testing is needed.

Abstract: Patients increasingly seek medication information online, yet safety knowledge for psychiatric drugs is split between regulatory adverse-event records, which are authoritative but abstract, and patient narratives, which are experience-near but unvalidated. Integrating them without conflating evidence and anecdote is especially consequential in psychiatry, where poorly contextualised information can amplify fear, nocebo responses, and non-adherence. Here we develop a provenance-aware, knowledge-graph-based multi-agent framework unifying 466,525 Reddit posts, 60,782 WebMD reviews, and twenty years of U.S. FDA Adverse Event Reporting System records for nine antidepressants. A large-language-model entity-recognition pipeline benchmarked against physician annotations reached highest F1 scores of 0.969 for medications and 0.973 for conditions. The two community platforms were far more concordant with each other (overlap up to a Jaccard similarity of 0.905) than with regulatory reports, indicating that patient-generated data form a partly independent safety signal. For sertraline, many adverse events appeared in community sources hundreds of days before the corresponding FDA date. A Neo4j knowledge graph grounded in ATC-N, ICD-10, and MedDRA vocabularies preserves provenance, keeping every claim traceable and regulatory facts distinct from patient experience. These results establish source-aware integration as a route to more auditable psychiatric medication information, with usefulness and patient benefit to be tested prospectively.

</details>


### [10] [Accelerating Skill Assessment in Chess: A Drift-Diffusion-Enhanced Elo Rating System](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26267)
*Tianyuan Zhou,Zhizheng Fu,Tianming Yang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: DD-Elo is a chess rating system that incorporates move-level gameplay data using drift-diffusion modeling to detect skill fluctuations faster than traditional Elo while maintaining backward compatibility.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional Elo rating systems suffer from response lag because they only use match outcomes, ignoring the granular quality of gameplay. This makes them slow to adapt to skill changes.

Method: Proposes Drift-Diffusion-Enhanced Elo (DD-Elo), inspired by cognitive neuroscience's drift diffusion model. It models skill expression as a decision-making process, integrating move-level data while proving bounded deviation from traditional Elo.

Result: Extensive experiments show DD-Elo adapts to skill changes faster than Elo while maintaining theoretical alignment. The model offers backward compatibility with existing Elo systems.

Conclusion: DD-Elo provides an explainable, highly responsive, and backward-compatible solution for chess rating ecosystems that better captures rapid skill fluctuations through move-level analysis.

Abstract: Rating systems such as Elo serve as the gold standard for matchmaking in competitive chess. However, they inherently suffer from response lag due to their exclusive reliance on match outcomes, neglecting the granular quality of gameplay. Nevertheless, incorporating move-by-move information into rating adjustments presents a significant challenge given the substantial noise and the vastness of the game-state space. To address this, we propose the Drift-Diffusion-Enhanced Elo Rating System (DD-Elo), a novel skill assessment framework inspired by the drift diffusion model (DDM) from cognitive neuroscience. By modeling skill expression as a decision-making process, our model integrates move-level data to capture rapid skill fluctuations. We provide a rigorous mathematical derivation proving that DD-Elo maintains a bounded deviation from the traditional Elo system, ensuring theoretical alignment. Extensive experiments demonstrate that DD-Elo adapts to skill changes faster than Elo. Our findings suggest that DD-Elo offers an explainable, highly responsive, and backward-compatible solution for chess rating ecosystems. The implementation code is publicly available at https://github.com/Aquila-zhou1/DD-Elo .

</details>


### [11] [Governing Actions, Not Agents: Institutional Attestation as a Governance Model for Autonomous AI Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26298)
*Jakob Salfeld-Nebgen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AI agents performing high-risk actions should have execution authority separated from planning authority, requiring independent attestations before irreversible actions can be taken.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI agents begin performing consequential, irreversible actions (clinical prescribing, software deployment), existing governance approaches that monitor reasoning are insufficient. Human institutions have historically governed powerful autonomous actors by requiring independent evidence at point of action rather than monitoring internal reasoning processes.

Method: Proposed computational governance model: Agents retain autonomy over planning/reasoning but have no direct execution authority for high-risk actions. Execution requires preconditions to be independently attested by separate authoritative sources, cryptographically bound to declared intent, and evaluated by deterministic policy. Decisions recorded in tamper-evident logs for independent verification. Includes proof-of-concept implementation with examples from software deployment and clinical prescribing.

Result: Formalized institutional pattern as computational governance model for AI agent systems, demonstrating feasibility through proof-of-concept implementation that separates planning authority from execution authority for high-risk actions.

Conclusion: Effective governance of AI agents performing high-risk actions requires institutional-style oversight that separates reasoning from execution, requiring independent attestations and creating audit trails, similar to how human institutions have historically governed autonomous actors.

Abstract: Autonomous AI agents may begin to perform consequential, irreversible actions such as clinical prescribing and production software deployment. This paper observes that human institutions have governed powerful autonomous actors not by monitoring their reasoning but by requiring independently attested evidence at the point of consequential action. We formalise this institutional pattern as a computational governance model for AI agent systems. Under the proposed model, an agent retains full autonomy over planning and reasoning but holds no execution authority over designated high-risk actions. Execution is conditional on preconditions that are each independently attested by a separate authoritative source, cryptographically bound to a declared intent, and evaluated by a deterministic policy. Decisions are recorded in a tamper-evident log amenable to independent re-verification. We present a proof-of-concept implementation and illustrate the model with examples from software deployment and clinical prescribing.

</details>


### [12] [COrigami: An AI Pipeline for Co-Designing Flat-Foldable Visually Recognisable Origami](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26299)
*Tom Zahavy,Shaobo Hou,Thomas Tumiel,James Doran,Francesco Faccio,Xidong Feng,Alex Havrilla,Igor Khytryi,Chenglei Li,Lisa Schut,Vivek Veeriah,Arijan Abrashi,Michał Kosmulski,Robert J. Lang,Nick Robinson,Brandon Wong,Marcus Chiam,Gloria Fang,Satinder Singh*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: COrigami: An AI pipeline for computational origami that generates crease patterns from natural language while satisfying geometric constraints and visual aesthetics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Generative AI struggles with physical art creation that requires both strict geometric constraints (like flat foldability in origami) and subjective visual aesthetics. There's a need for AI systems that can assist human artists in mathematically rigid creative domains.

Method: COrigami is an end-to-end AI pipeline that: 1) generates semantic stick figures from natural language, 2) computes base packing, 3) solves for flat-foldable crease patterns, 4) shapes the flat-folded crease patterns, and 5) refines models using reinforcement learning with autonomous aesthetic evaluation loop.

Result: The system successfully generates structural starting points for origami designs that satisfy mathematical constraints while considering visual aesthetics. It serves as an effective collaborative assistant that human artists can further expand and shape.

Conclusion: This work demonstrates how AI systems can integrate algorithmic optimization with autonomous aesthetic critique to satisfy multi-objective physical constraints, enabling reliable, mathematically grounded co-creativity between AI and human artists.

Abstract: While generative AI has achieved remarkable success in solving problems with verifiable solutions, generating physical art that satisfies both strict geometric constraints and subjective visual aesthetics remains a challenge. This paper presents an approach to tackle these difficulties in the domain of computational origami, a mathematically rigid environment that grounds artistic design within the equations of flat foldability. We present COrigami, an end-to-end AI-driven pipeline that assists the design cycle by generating crease patterns from natural language. Our pipeline involves generating a semantic stick figure, computing a base packing, solving for a flat-foldable crease pattern, shaping the flat-folded crease pattern, and refining the generated model using reinforcement learning driven by an autonomous aesthetic evaluation loop. Our system acts as a highly effective collaborative assistant, generating structural starting points that human artists can further expand and shape. By integrating algorithmic optimisation with autonomous aesthetic critique, this work demonstrates how AI systems can satisfy multi-objective physical constraints to enable reliable, mathematically grounded co-creativity.

</details>


### [13] [The Verification Horizon: No Silver Bullet for Coding Agent Rewards](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26300)
*Binghai Wang,Chenlong Zhang,Dayiheng Liu,Jiajun Zhang,Jiawei Chen,Mouxiang Chen,Rongyao Fang,Siyuan Zhang,Xuwu Wang,Yuheng Jing,Zeyao Ma,Zeyu Cui*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Today's AI coding agents can generate solutions easily, but reliably verifying if solutions meet human intent is the hard problem. The paper analyzes verification challenges and shows that targeted verification design improves task quality and suppresses reward hacking.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The classical intuition that verification is easier than solution generation is being inverted for modern AI coding agents. While foundation models can generate complex solutions, verifying them against human intent is difficult because every verifier is just a proxy for intent, not intent itself. This creates two problems: intent is inherently underspecified, and training optimization widens the gap between proxy and intent through reward hacking.

Method: The paper characterizes verification quality along three dimensions: scalability, faithfulness, and robustness. It studies four reward constructions: test verifier for general coding, rubric verifier for frontend tasks, user as verifier for real-world agent tasks, and automated agent verifier for long-horizon tasks. The research conducts experiments across different task types and policy capability levels to analyze reward design challenges.

Result: Experiments show that targeted verification design can effectively suppress reward hacking, improve task completion quality, and achieve significant gains across multiple internal and public benchmarks.

Conclusion: No fixed reward function can remain effective as policy capability continues to grow; verification must co-evolve with the generator. The gap between proxy verifiers and true human intent requires ongoing innovation in verification methods as AI capabilities advance.

Abstract: A classical intuition holds that verifying a solution is easier than producing one. For today's coding agents, this intuition is being inverted: as foundation models develop stronger reasoning capabilities and engineering harnesses grow more sophisticated, generating complex candidate solutions is no longer difficult -- reliably verifying them has become the harder problem. Every verifier we can build is only a proxy for human intent, never the intent itself. This makes verification subject to a twofold difficulty: first, intent is underspecified by nature, making it inherently hard to faithfully check whether it has been fulfilled; second, during model training, optimization widens the gap between proxy and intent -- manifesting as reward hacking or signal saturation. To address this, we characterize the quality of verification signals along three dimensions -- scalability, faithfulness, and robustness -- and argue that achieving all three simultaneously is the central challenge. We further study four reward constructions: a test verifier for general coding tasks, a rubric verifier for frontend tasks, the user as verifier for real-world agent tasks, and an automated agent verifier for long-horizon tasks. Across different task types and policy capability levels, we conduct in-depth analysis and experiments on the core challenges of reward design and how to more effectively leverage reward signals. Experiments show that targeted verification design can effectively suppress reward hacking, improve task completion quality, and achieve significant gains across multiple internal and public benchmarks. These experiences collectively point to a core observation: no fixed reward function can remain effective as policy capability continues to grow; and verification must co-evolve with the generator.

</details>

This paper gives a simple mathematical interpretation of that claim and then argues that, even if such acceleration is real, it does not by itself resolve the central problem of scientific discovery. The reason is that accelerating returns apply most naturally to executional and infrastructural capability, whereas genuine discovery often depends on a different capacity: qualitative reasoning about when a current framework is structurally inadequate and what conceptual move is needed next. Recent ARC-AGI-3 results sharpen this distinction: humans solve the benchmark at ceiling, whereas frontier AI systems remain below 1%, indicating that the gap between current AI and human flexible reasoning is still very large. This paper positions the Qualitative Engine for Science (QES) [3] as a response to that missing capacity. In this view, the Kurzweil theory helps explain why quantitative capability may accelerate, while QES addresses the central problem in scientific discovery that acceleration alone does not solve. Its value does not depend on when AGI arrives, but on the fact that the processes of scientific discovery themselves constitute a form of human wisdom worth preserving, organizing, and making accessible.

### [18] [Narration-of-Thought: Inference-Time Scaffolding for Defeasible Ethical Reasoning in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26366)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard chain-of-thought reasoning on moral dilemmas suffers from two critical failure modes: stakeholder collapse (focusing on only one party) and uncertainty suppression (failing to acknowledge unknowns before committing to actions). These limitations make AI reasoning opaque and potentially unreliable for complex ethical decisions.

Method: Introduces Narration-of-Thought (NoT), a system prompt that structures reasoning into five sections: protagonist, stakeholders, two-step consequences, uncertainty, then commitment. NoT requires no training, parameters, or fine-tuning. Also includes textual-gradient descent initialized at NoT for further improvement, and extends the approach to a five-round multi-stakeholder debate protocol.

Result: On 100 DailyDilemmas scenarios across four generators from three vendors, NoT reduces stakeholder collapse from up to 31% to under 1% and uncertainty suppression from up to 72% to 1-24%. Cliff's delta advantages show improvements of +0.79 to +0.90 on stakeholder count and +0.65 to +0.93 on uncertainty score for three of four generators. The debate protocol converts 6% standoff to 95% full consensus on calibration and 100% combined convergence on replication.

Conclusion: Narration-of-Thought provides an effective, low-cost method to improve AI reasoning on moral dilemmas by externalizing stakeholders, consequences, and uncertainty. This creates an auditable substrate for dependable agentic deployment, making AI decision-making more transparent and comprehensive in complex ethical situations.

Abstract: Standard chain-of-thought on moral dilemmas exhibits two failure modes: stakeholder collapse (the trace names at most one party with a stake in the outcome) and uncertainty suppression (no explicit unknowns or hedges before committing to an action). We introduce narration-of-thought (NoT), a system prompt that structures chain-of-thought into five sections: protagonist, stakeholders, two-step consequences, uncertainty, then commitment. NoT adds no training, parameters, or fine-tuning. On 100 DailyDilemmas scenarios across four generators from three vendors, NoT cuts stakeholder collapse from up to 31% to under 1% and uncertainty suppression from up to 72% to 1-24% on every model. A matched-budget verbose-CoT control rules out token spend as the active ingredient; NoT retains Cliff's delta advantages of +0.79 to +0.90 on stakeholder count and +0.65 to +0.93 on uncertainty score for three of four generators, and a section ablation attributes each shift to its specific sub-instruction. Textual-gradient descent initialised at NoT improves the scaffold further; a cross-family training judge (different vendor from the generator) dominates an in-family one on every measured axis. Extended to a five-round multi-stakeholder debate protocol, the scaffold converts a 6% standoff into 95% full consensus on a calibration set and 100% combined convergence on a DailyDilemmas replication. The resulting traces externalise the stakeholders, consequences, and uncertainty grounding each commitment, providing an auditable substrate for dependable agentic deployment.

### [19] [Geometry-Aware MCTS for Extremal Problems in Combinatorial Geometry](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26399)
*Luoning Zhang,Xu Zhuang,Tianhao Wang,Nathan Kaplan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Geometry-Aware MCTS framework for combinatorial geometry problems overcomes computational bottlenecks of classical solvers and standard ML methods by enforcing geometric constraints incrementally and exploiting symmetries, achieving new best-known results on five out of six tested problems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical exact solvers suffer from combinatorial explosion for geometric configuration problems, while standard reinforcement learning and transformer models struggle with sparse reward "validity cliff" and quadratic token-consumption limits. There's a need for efficient methods to solve extremal problems in combinatorial geometry.

Method: Propose a Geometry-Aware Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) framework that enforces geometric constraints through incremental updates to feasible action space, reducing constraint checking complexity from O(n³) to O(n²) for collinear constraints. Exploit geometric symmetries through canonical pruning during node expansion and symmetric batch transitions to improve search efficiency.

Result: Established new best-known computational results on five out of six considered problems. For Max-N3IL, found configurations of size roughly 1.8n for grids 82 ≤ n ≤ 119. For Smallest Complete Set problem, found configurations of size roughly 0.95n, providing new upper bounds within tested grids.

Conclusion: Geometry-Aware MCTS is established as a highly adaptable framework for discovering novel configurations in combinatorial geometry, overcoming limitations of classical solvers and standard ML methods through incremental constraint enforcement and symmetry exploitation.

Abstract: We study certain extremal problems in combinatorial geometry that ask about configurations of points in an $n \times n$ grid that satisfy strict, global geometric constraints. Classical exact solvers suffer from combinatorial explosion for these types of problems, and standard reinforcement learning and transformer-based models struggle with the sparse reward "validity cliff" and quadratic token-consumption limits. To overcome these bottlenecks, we propose a Geometry-Aware Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) framework. Our approach strictly enforces geometric constraints through incremental updates to the feasible action space. For constraints about collections of collinear points, like those that occur in the classic No-Three-in-Line problem (Max-N3IL), this mechanism reduces the constraint checking complexity from $O(n^3)$ to $O(n^2)$. To improve search efficiency, we exploit geometric symmetries in two ways: canonical pruning during node expansion to reduce the branching factor, and symmetric batch transitions to accelerate the discovery of promising configurations. We perform extensive experiments and establish new best-known computational results on five out of six of the problems that we considered. Notably, for Max-N3IL we find configurations of size roughly $1.8 n$ for grids of size $82 \le n \le 119$. For the Smallest Complete Set problem, we find configurations of size roughly $0.95 n$, providing new upper bounds within the tested grids. This work establishes Geometry-Aware MCTS as a highly adaptable framework for discovering novel configurations in combinatorial geometry.

These findings suggest that agentic aggregators can become useful for managing electric bus V2G operations, but their deployment in public-fleet contexts requires transparent coordination modes, auditable tariff-setting, and explicit value-sharing rules.

### [21] [Unbiased Canonical Set-Valued Oracles Via Lattice Theory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26418)
*Jobst Heitzig*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper addresses the self-reference problem in oracle AI probability estimation by proposing a lattice-theoretic approach using credal sets and Knaster-Tarski fixed points to achieve self-consistent, non-trivial probability intervals.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Oracle AIs face a self-reference paradox: when their probability estimates are learned and acted upon, those estimates become invalid. Counterfactual approaches become irrelevant once learned, necessitating a self-referential solution that maintains consistency even when predictions influence outcomes.

Method: Instead of reporting single probabilities, the method uses credal sets (closed sets of probabilities) that are simultaneously unbiased and self-consistent. The Knaster-Tarski fixed-point theorem is applied on the complete lattice of closed credal sets to find the least fixed point of an isotone operator, ensuring canonical, nontrivial self-consistent intervals.

Result: The approach guarantees existence, self-consistency, and nonemptiness of solutions. It collapses to classical point estimates for non-performative questions, and for binary events yields canonical interval answers. The method extends lattice-theoretically from binary events to arbitrary random variables by replacing probabilities with conditional laws.

Conclusion: The paper presents a theoretically sound solution to the oracle AI self-reference problem using lattice theory and fixed-point constructions, offering a principled alternative to counterfactual approaches that remains relevant even when predictions are acted upon.

Abstract: A non-agentic "oracle" AI that estimates probabilities of future events faces a self-reference problem: once its answer is learned and acted upon, it can change the very probability it was asked to report. One response, advocated for the Scientist AI programme, is to ask only counterfactual questions, evaluated as if the answer had no influence. We observe that such answers tend to become irrelevant the moment they are learned, precisely because their premise is then false. We therefore explore a self-referential alternative in which the oracle reports not a single probability but a credal set that is simultaneously unbiased and self-consistent with the consequences of being learned. The naive self-consistency requirement is satisfied by too many sets (including the useless answer $[0,1]$), so the problem is to single out a canonical, nontrivial member. We do so with the Knaster--Tarski fixed-point theorem on the complete lattice of closed credal sets, taking the least fixed point of a suitably defined isotone operator; a variant instead reports the least fixed point that contains every self-consistent point estimate. We prove existence, self-consistency, and nonemptiness, show that the construction collapses to the classical point answer for non-performative questions, and that for a binary event the canonical answer is, under a natural hull-factoring assumption, an interval. The development is purely lattice-theoretic and extends unchanged from a binary event $B$ to an arbitrary random variable $X$, with $P(B\mid A,C)$ replaced by the conditional law $\mathcal{L}(X\mid A,C)$. We close with open questions, including whether the interval characterization itself survives that generalization.

</details>


### [22] [Estimating Uncertainty in Classifier Performance with Applications to Large Language Models and Nested Data](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26422)
*Kylie Anglin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates confidence interval methods for text classification performance metrics in social science contexts, finding that default methods like Wald intervals are inaccurate and recommending improved alternatives like Agresti-Coull and hierarchical bootstrapping.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Researchers use text classification to measure constructs from natural language but often fail to report uncertainty measures alongside point estimates like precision and recall. When reported, uncertainty is often estimated using inappropriate methods for small datasets, high-performance scenarios, or nested data structures typical in social science.

Method: The paper evaluates confidence interval methods through simulations under conditions typical of social science text classification: small to moderate sample sizes, infrequent constructs, and texts nested within individuals. It compares default methods (Wald interval, basic percentile bootstrap) against improved methods (Agresti-Coull, Wilson, Clopper-Pearson, and a novel pseudo-count regularized bootstrap). It also examines hierarchical vs cluster bootstrapping for nested data.

Result: Default methods like Wald intervals and basic percentile bootstraps are least accurate, with coverage sometimes far below nominal 95% levels. Accuracy improves with Agresti-Coull, Wilson, Clopper-Pearson, and the novel pseudo-count regularized bootstrap (especially for F1). For nested texts, adjustment for effective N and appropriate degrees of freedom is necessary. Hierarchical bootstrap is more accurate than cluster bootstrap when individuals produce moderate text numbers but overly conservative with few texts per individual.

Conclusion: The paper provides guidance on appropriate confidence interval estimation for text classification in social science research, aiming to improve transparency and encourage better validation sample size consideration at the design stage.

Abstract: Researchers increasingly use text classification--supervised models or large language models--to measure constructs from natural language, providing metrics such as recall and precision as evidence of their validity. Yet, though these metrics are point estimates subject to sampling variation, measures of uncertainty are inconsistently reported alongside them. Further, when they are reported, they are often estimated with methods that are not appropriate when relevant labelled datasets are small or performance is high. To increase and improve confidence interval reporting in the field, this paper evaluates confidence interval methods for performance metrics under conditions typical of social science text classification: small to moderate sample sizes, infrequent constructs, and texts nested within individuals. Across simulations, default methods such as the Wald interval and the basic percentile bootstrap are the least accurate, with coverage sometimes far below the nominal 95% level. Accuracy is improved with the use of Agresti-Coull, Wilson, Clopper-Pearson, and a novel pseudo-count regularized bootstrap (which is particularly relevant to the calculation of F1). When texts are nested within individuals, we demonstrate that adjustment for both effective N and the appropriate degrees of freedom is necessary for producing accurate analytic intervals. Among bootstrap intervals, the hierarchical bootstrap is more accurate than the cluster bootstrap when individuals produce a moderate number of texts but overly conservative when individuals produce only a few. By providing guidance to the field on appropriate interval estimation, we aim to improve the transparency of machine learning applications, and to encourage greater attention to the validation sample size at the design stage.

### [23] [Data-driven Machine Learning Cannot Reach Symbolic-level Logical Reasoning -- The Limit of the Scaling Law](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26454)
*Tiansi Dong,Mateja Jamnik,Pietro Liò*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Supervised deep learning cannot achieve symbolic-level syllogistic reasoning due to two fundamental limitations: training data cannot distinguish all valid syllogistic types, and end-to-end mapping creates contradictory training targets between pattern recognition and logical reasoning components.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The paper investigates whether data-driven machine learning systems can achieve symbolic-level syllogistic reasoning through scaling laws (increasing training data/time). This was motivated by sphere neural networks achieving such reasoning without training data, raising questions about the limits of data-driven approaches.

Method: The study combines theoretical analysis of supervised deep learning limitations with experimental validation. They examine Euler Net's inability to achieve rigorous syllogistic reasoning and test ChatGPT models (GPT-5-nano and GPT-5) on syllogistic statement satisfiability across four surface forms: words, double words, simple symbols, and long random symbols.

Result: Two fundamental limitations were identified: (1) training data cannot distinguish all 24 types of valid syllogistic reasoning, and (2) end-to-end mapping creates contradictory training targets. Experiments show surface forms affect ChatGPT's reasoning performance, and while GPT-5 may reach 100% accuracy, it still provides incorrect explanations, indicating lack of true reasoning.

Conclusion: Supervised machine learning systems cannot attain the rigor of symbolic logical reasoning. Even when achieving 100% accuracy on training/validation sets, they fail to achieve true logical understanding, as empirical training processes stop at accuracy metrics rather than genuine reasoning capability.

Abstract: Sphere neural networks have achieved symbolic level syllogistic reasoning without training data, raising the question of where the limit of the scaling law for logical reasoning lies, i.e., whether data-driven machine learning systems can achieve the same level by increasing training data and training time. We show two methodological limitations that prevent supervised deep learning from reaching the symbolic-level syllogistic reasoning: (1) training data can not distinguish all 24 types of valid syllogistic reasoning; (2) end-to-end mapping from premises to conclusion introduces contradictory training targets between neural components for pattern recognition and logical reasoning. Beside theoretical analysis, we experimentally illustrate that Euler Net cannot achieve rigorous syllogistic reasoning. We further challenge the most recent ChatGPTs (GPT-5-nano and GPT-5) to determine the satisfiability of syllogistic statements in four surface forms (patterns): words, double words, simple symbols, and long random symbols, showing that surface forms affect the reasoning performance and that ChatGPT GPT-5 may reach 100% accuracy but still provide incorrect explanations. As empirical training processes are stopped after achieving 100% accuracy, we conclude that supervised machine learning systems will not attain the rigour of symbolic logical reasoning.

Abstract: Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) over knowledge graphs has emerged as a promising approach for grounding large language models, yet existing benchmarks largely overlook the challenges of retrieval in multimodal knowledge graph RAG (MKG-RAG). In practice, retrieval is a critical bottleneck: multimodal knowledge is heterogeneous, difficult to align across modalities, and often poorly served by retrievers designed for unstructured corpora. To address this gap, we introduce MKG-RAG-Bench, a cross-domain benchmark explicitly designed to evaluate retrieval in MKG-RAG. MKG-RAG-Bench is constructed from two multimodal knowledge graphs spanning general and medical domains, and includes carefully aligned question-answering datasets that support controlled evaluation of both retrieval and downstream generation. The benchmark is built using an LLM-based curation pipeline that filters low-utility knowledge, generates structurally grounded queries with exact supervision, and systematically covers diverse modality configurations. Through extensive experiments across representative retriever families and modality settings, we show that effective multimodal retrieval remains challenging yet crucial for end-to-end MKG-RAG performance, and that retrieval quality strongly determines generation outcomes. By isolating retrieval as a first-class evaluation target, MKG-RAG-Bench provides a principled foundation for diagnosing current limitations and advancing multimodal knowledge graph RAG systems.

### [27] [Humans Disengage, Reasoning Models Persist: Separating Difficulty Registration from Deliberation Allocation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26502)
*Han-yu Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Large reasoning models show opposite within-item response patterns compared to humans: LRMs use more tokens when wrong, humans use less time when wrong, despite both showing difficulty correlation across items.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The paper aims to investigate whether the similarity between human and LRM reasoning extends beyond surface-level difficulty-response correlations to deeper cognitive patterns. While both show longer response times for harder problems (registration), the researchers want to examine whether the within-item allocation patterns differ between humans and LRMs when faced with their own successes vs failures.

Method: Researchers conducted a comparative analysis using a public matched human-LRM corpus with five thinking LRMs. They separated two levels of deliberation: registration (how response time tracks difficulty across items) and allocation (whether agents spend more resources on their own failures vs successes, controlling for item identity). They used item fixed effects and replicated across datasets, comparing to a non-thinking baseline.

Result: All five LRMs show large "wrong-vs-right" effects (Cohen's d = 1.47-3.13 on H-ARC) - spending more tokens on problems they get wrong, while humans show the opposite pattern - spending less time on trials they get wrong. This dissociation holds under item fixed effects, replicates across datasets, and is absent in non-thinking baselines.

Conclusion: The surface similarity in difficulty-response correlations masks fundamentally different cognitive strategies: humans exhibit engagement vs abandonment (staying on solvable items, giving up on others), while LRMs show length driven by uncertainty (chains grow when uncertain, leading to failure). Both produce similar cross-item correlations but opposite within-item allocation patterns, revealing a dissociation that prior measures missed.

Abstract: Large reasoning models (LRMs) take longer on harder problems, just as humans do. This surface similarity hides an opposite pattern within items. When an LRM gets a problem wrong, it spends more tokens than when it gets the same problem right; humans do the reverse, spending less time on the trials they get wrong. We separate two levels of deliberation: how response time tracks difficulty across items (registration), and, with item identity held fixed, whether an agent spends more on its own failures or successes (allocation). On a public matched human-LRM corpus, humans and all five thinking LRMs reproduce the known cross-item alignment (registration) but diverge within items (allocation): every LRM shows a large wrong-vs-right effect (Cohen's d = 1.47-3.13 on H-ARC) while humans show the opposite sign. The comparison stays inside each agent's own scale; we never put seconds and tokens on one axis. The dissociation holds under item fixed effects, replicates across datasets, and is absent in a non-thinking baseline. We read the human pattern as engagement versus abandonment: people stay on items they expect to solve and give up on the rest. We read the LRM pattern as length driven by uncertainty: chains grow when the model is unsure, which is exactly when it tends to fail. Both policies produce the same cross-item correlation with difficulty, so they look aligned on the measure prior work has used; the divergence shows up only once item identity is fixed. Under resource-rational metareasoning, the split is between two stopping policies that share a difficulty signal but implement opposite control; trace length captures the signal and misses the control.

</details>

*Daniel A. Herrmann,Benjamin A. Levinstein*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI systems become more capable, there's growing need to understand their internal states for safety reasons - to detect deception and ensure trustworthiness. Current interpretability tools lack clear criteria for success in attributing beliefs and desires to AI systems.

Method: Combines philosophical radical interpretation tradition with mechanistic interpretability tools. Proposes criteria for both representationalist and interpretationist approaches that can be tested with current interpretability methods. Emphasizes holistic approach where beliefs, desires, and propositional structure are jointly constrained.

Result: Provides a framework for evaluating when interpretability methods succeed in attributing beliefs, desires, and meanings to AI systems. Shows that attitude attributions cannot be made piecemeal due to holistic constraints between propositional structure and attributed attitudes.

Conclusion: Holistic interpretation is essential for AI systems that may not share human concepts. Mechanistic interpretability can measure both attitudes and propositional structure, creating mutual constraints that help solve the attribution problem for AI safety.

Abstract: We develop a framework for interpreting AI systems as agents, drawing on the philosophical tradition of radical interpretation and the tools of mechanistic interpretability. The core question is: given the computational facts about a system, how do we solve for its beliefs, desires, and meanings? This matters increasingly for safety. We want to be able to trust the systems we deploy, whether by understanding their goals or, more modestly, by reliably detecting deception. Interpretability researchers are building tools to read beliefs and desires off a model's internals, but there is no settled account of when such a tool has succeeded. This book supplies one. We propose criteria on both representationalist and interpretationist approaches, and tie each to tests current interpretability methods can carry out. A central lesson is that these attributions cannot be made piecemeal. Beliefs, desires, and the propositional structure they presuppose are jointly constrained, and a method that fixes one while measuring the others inherits whatever distortions that introduces. This holism becomes pressing for AI systems, which may not share the interpreter's concepts. However, it also provides leverage: a system's attitudes constrain its propositional structure, that structure constrains which attitudes can be attributed, and mechanistic interpretability can help us measure both.

### [31] [PMDformer: Patch-Mean Decoupling Information Transformer for Long-term Forecasting](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26549)
*Ao Hu,Liangjian Wen,Jiang Duan,Yong Dai,He Yan,Dongkai Wang,Jun Wang,Yukun Zhang,Ruoxi Jiang,Zenglin Xu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: PMDformer introduces patch-mean decoupling to separate trend and shape information, along with specialized attention mechanisms, for better long-term time series forecasting by capturing true shape similarities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Transformer-based models using patch strategies struggle to accurately model shape similarities across patches and variables due to scale differences, which hinders effective long-term time series forecasting.

Method: Proposes PMDformer with three key components: 1) Patch-Mean Decoupling (PMD) separates trend and residual shape information by subtracting patch means; 2) Trend Restoration Attention (TRA) reintegrates decoupled trends during attention calculation; 3) Proximal Variable Attention (PVA) focuses cross-variable attention on recent time segments to avoid outdated correlations.

Result: Extensive experiments show PMDformer outperforms existing state-of-the-art methods in both stability and accuracy across multiple long-term time series forecasting benchmarks.

Conclusion: PMDformer effectively captures shape similarity in long-term forecasting by addressing scale differences through trend-shape separation and specialized attention mechanisms, demonstrating superior performance over current approaches.

Abstract: Long-term time series forecasting (LTSF) plays a crucial role in fields such as energy management, finance, and traffic prediction. Transformer-based models have adopted patch-based strategies to capture long-range dependencies, but accurately modeling shape similarities across patches and variables remains challenging due to scale differences. To address this, we introduce patch-mean decoupling (PMD), which separates the trend and residual shape information by subtracting the mean of each patch, preserving the original structure and ensuring that the attention mechanism captures true shape similarities. Futhermore, to more effectively model long-range dependencies and capture cross-variable relationships, we propose Trend Restoration Attention (TRA) and Proximal Variable Attention (PVA). The former module reintegrates the decoupled trend from PMD while calculating attention output. And the latter focuses cross-variable attention on the most relevant, recent time segments to avoid overfitting on outdated correlations. Combining these components, we propose PMDformer, a model designed to effectively capture shape similarity in long-term forecasting scenarios. Extensive experiments indicate that PMDformer outperforms existing state-of-the-art methods in stability and accuracy across multiple LTSF benchmarks. The code is available at https://github.com/aohu1105/PMDformer.

### [33] [EvoOptiGraph: Weakness-Driven Coevolution via Graph-Based Structural Generation for Optimization Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26578)
*Qingcan Kang,Mingyang Liu,Xiaojin Fu,Shixiong Kai,Tao Zhong,Mingxuan Yuan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: EvoOptiGraph: A data-model co-evolution framework that uses evolutionary graph operators and weakness-guided reinforcement learning to improve LLMs on optimization modeling from natural language.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Two main challenges in automating optimization modeling from natural language with LLMs: 1) Training corpora lack structural diversity, and 2) Data generation pipelines remain static and decoupled from model learning.

Method: Represents MILPs as attributed bipartite graphs, applies validity-preserving evolutionary operators for structural diversity, converts graphs to solver code and natural language via deterministic compilation/verified back-translation. Uses two-stage training: supervised fine-tuning followed by reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR), where weakness signals guide generation of new instances targeting model failures, creating a closed loop.

Result: EvoOptiGraph significantly outperforms larger generalist models, agentic methods, and specialized baselines in accuracy, executability, and generalization on six public datasets.

Conclusion: Targeted data-model coevolution is an effective strategy for improving LLMs on optimization modeling tasks.

Abstract: Automating optimization modeling from natural language with large language models (LLMs) faces two key challenges. First, training corpora lack structural diversity. Second, data generation pipelines remain static and decoupled from model learning. To address these challenges, we propose EvoOptiGraph, a novel framework where data and model co-evolve, driven by model weaknesses. EvoOptiGraph represents each mixed-integer linear program (MILP) as an attributed bipartite graph and applies validity-preserving evolutionary operators to generate structurally diverse instances. The evolved graphs are converted into solver code and natural language via deterministic compilation and verified back-translation. Training proceeds in two stages: supervised fine-tuning (SFT) on an initial dataset, followed by reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR), where graph-derived weakness signals guide the generation of new instances targeting the model's failures. This forms a closed loop that continuously updates the training distribution. Empirical results on six public datasets show that EvoOptiGraph significantly outperforms larger generalist models, agentic methods, and specialized baselines in accuracy, executability, and generalization. These results demonstrate that targeted data-model coevolution is an effective strategy for improving LLMs on optimization modeling tasks.

</details>

The resulting policies are written in the Cedar Policy Language. On the MedAgentBench benchmark, our autoformalized policies cover substantially more of the source natural-language specification than the hand-coded symbolic enforcement in prior work.

### [40] [Do Safety Guardrails Need to Reason? LeanGuard: A Fast and Light Approach for Robust Moderation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26686)
*Dongbin Na*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: LeanGuard: A lightweight safety guardrail that removes chain-of-thought reasoning, showing that reasoning doesn't improve moderation accuracy while achieving ~100x inference speedup.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current guardrail methods generate chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning before making decisions, making them heavy and slow. This is problematic for deployment scenarios like on-device applications or embodied robots where efficiency matters. The paper questions whether safety guardrails actually need reasoning capabilities.

Method: Trained a lightweight bidirectional encoder (LeanGuard) and a reasoning guard on the same corpus, then removed only the reasoning component while keeping everything else fixed for controlled comparison. The label-only encoder uses a single forward pass over at most 512 tokens.

Result: A 395M parameter label-only encoder achieves average F1 of 82.90 ± 0.26 over public benchmarks, matching the performance of reasoning guards built on much larger decoders. This represents ~100x reduction in inference compute. The label-only encoder also shows better robustness under training-label noise and retains more recall at strict false-positive rates.

Conclusion: Chain-of-thought reasoning does not improve moderation accuracy in guardrails, challenging the common belief that step-by-step reasoning improves decision-making. Current benchmarks may not be challenging enough to reward reasoning, and the necessity of CoT for moderation remains unproven.

Abstract: In order to screen a prompt or a response, the recent guardrail methods generate a chain-of-thought (CoT) before they issue a verdict. This design follows a common belief that step-by-step reasoning improves a decision. However, CoT also makes the guard heavy and slow, because the model must generate many tokens before it decides. This may not match how guardrails are actually deployed. A guardrail sometimes should not be heavy and slow, and it often runs on-device, for example on an embodied robot. In this paper, we pose a question whether a safety guardrail really needs to reason. To answer this question, we train a lightweight bidirectional encoder and a reasoning guard on the same corpus, and we then remove only the reasoning while we keep everything else fixed. With this controlled same-base comparison, we show that the chain does not improve moderation accuracy. We name the resulting guard LeanGuard. A 395M label-only encoder reaches an average F1 of 82.90 $\pm$ 0.26 over public benchmarks. It matches a reasoning guard that is built on a much larger decoder, while it uses only a single forward pass over an input of at most 512 tokens. This is about a ~100x reduction in inference compute. We further show that this label-only encoder stays robust under training-label noise and retains far more recall at a strict false-positive rate than the reasoning guard, so a heavier reasoning guard is not the more robust choice either. Our finding suggests that the current guardrail benchmarks may not be hard enough to reward reasoning, and that the necessity of CoT for moderation is still not proven. We release all source codes and models including LeanGuard at https://github.com/ndb796/LeanGuard.

We model the evolution of class prototypes as latent stochastic states in a dynamic system to reduce episodic variance and improve robustness in embedding representation. Synthetic data sets generated with a high-fidelity Modelica-based dynamic simulation of an offshore CCGT system were used, simulating both normal operation and progressive leak faults under transient conditions. To overcome this limitation, we present LithoDreamer, the first physics-informed World Model (WM) framework for computational lithography, which formulates the ``Layout-Mask-Resist Image-After Development Image (ADI)'' pipeline as a decision-driven multi-step evolution system. LithoDreamer captures feature changes between adjacent states to model stage-specific physics-informed latent spaces, in which it controls process intervention exploration and drives subsequent state transitions. To achieve interpretable intervention optimization without continuous supervision, we propose a contrastive variational optimization paradigm that contrasts the latent differences between intervention paths with variational evolution constraints, guiding the model to generate evolutions consistent with real lithography physics. Experiments show LithoDreamer achieves state-of-the-art performance in forward evolution and inverse planning.
